Fig. 2.1.2. ORNL Data on the NaF-ThF<sub>4</sub> System.

The Russian diagram shows a congruently melting compound NaF·ThF<sub>4</sub>. ORNL data show that the compound NaF·ThF<sub>4</sub> melts incongruity to NaF·2ThF<sub>4</sub> and liquid.

Unclassified Russian reports presently represent the predominant number of contributions to the literature of phase equilibrium studies of fused salt mixtures. Since 1946 more than 200 Russian reports have been issued concerning the phase equilibria of oxide, silicate, chloride, nitrate, and fluoride systems.

Most Russian phase work is done with thermal analysis and differential thermal analysis techniques. Phase identification is accomplished for the most part with x-ray diffraction. No reports

indicate the use of the thermal-gradient quenching techniques or of routine petrographic examinations.

# THE SYSTEM NaF-CrF2

H.A.Friedman C.F.Weaver

Phase equilibria in the NaF-structural metal fluoride systems NaF-CrF $_2$ , NaF-FeF $_2$ , and NaF-NiF $_2$ have been studied in an effort to elucidate the corrosion process as it is related to these materials. Phase diagrams for the systems NaF-FeF $_2$ and NaF-NiF $_2$ have been reported. $^7$

No fused-salt melts from thermal analysis experiments or from thermal-gradient quenching experiments have produced pure $\mathsf{NaF - CrF}_2$ samples that are free of $\mathbf{Cr}^{+ + + }$ . A variety of means has been

employed to minimize the effects of the disproportionation reaction

$$
3 \mathrm {C r F} _ {2} \rightleftharpoons 2 \mathrm {C r F} _ {3} + \mathrm {C r} ^ {\circ}
$$

in these phase equilibrium studies. Even the inclusion of 20 to 30 wt % chromium metal powder dispersed through samples for thermal-gradient quenching has not suppressed the forward reaction sufficiently for NaF-CrF $_2$ phase equilibrium data to be derived.

# APPARATUS FOR HIGH-TEMPERATURE THERMAL-GRADIENT QUENCHING EXPERIMENTS

H.A.Friedman

A design modification of the apparatus $^{8,9}$ used in thermal-gradient quenching experiments has permitted the feasible upper operating temperature limit to be raised from 850 to $1300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Previous difficulties encountered with quenching apparatus when operated routinely at 900 to $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ included the frequent need for replacement of the 18 thermocouples which monitored the thermal gradient, the rupture of the sample tube because of oxidation corrosion, uncertainties as to temperature constancy of the sample because of discontinuous monitoring, and sample tube expansion during heating.

In the revised apparatus a single, traveling, recording thermocouple was substituted for the 18 monitoring thermocouples. The furnace was redesigned to permit the sample tubes to be protected by a static helium atmosphere during heating. The use of a roller crimping machine contributes to minimizing sample tube expansion.

The apparatus, shown schematically in Fig. 2.1.3, now consists of a round nickel block 10 in. in length and 2 in. in diameter, which is supported in a $2\frac{1}{2}$ -in. tube furnace that has a platinum wound core. Interchangeable gears allow a choice of excursion times for the traveling thermocouple. A complete thermocouple travel cycle of 2 hr is usually employed in using this apparatus.

The roller and crimping machine (Fig. 2.1.4) is designed so that a 6-in.-long, 0.10-in.-OD, 0.010-in.-wall nickel tube containing a sample

to be quenched is flattened and crimped at $\frac{1}{4}$ -in. intervals. The sample tubes are sealed by welding after they have been rolled and crimped. Sample tubes prepared in this manner have better heat transfer characteristics than those of the tubes used previously. Much less expansion of the tubes during heating occurs because the void space is also reduced in the crimping process.

# PETROGRAPHIC ANALYSES OF FUEL MIXTURES

R.A. Strehlow

The petrographic microscope has been used on a routine basis for the inspection of solidified fuel mixtures. This type of examination is performed to detect the presence of oxygen-containing compounds and, for some samples, to determine any variance from the nominal composition. Mixtures of NaF-ZrF₄, NaF-ZrF₄-UF₄, LiF-NaF-KF-UF₄, and related compositions have formed the bulk of samples submitted for inspection. An illustration of the results obtained with the technique used in the inspection of these samples is given here for NaF-ZrF₄ mixtures containing about 50% ZrF₄.

Mixtures in the NaF-ZrF $_4$ system which contain between 46.2 and 57.1 mole $\%$ ZrF $_4$ will contain some 3NaF-4ZrF $_4$ which will, in general, occur in the slowly cooled melt and will be dispersed in eutectic aggregates that also contain 7NaF-6ZrF $_4$ . The aggregate frequently will exhibit a brown cast, which has been attributed to hydration of 3NaF-4ZrF $_4$ . Better crystallized 3NaF-4ZrF $_4$ has been observed in samples containing about 56 mole $\%$ ZrF $_4$ . The material present as the matrix in the eutectic aggregates is the compound with the reported formula of 7NaF-6ZrF $_4$ .

This compound occurs as the primary phase in mixtures containing from 46.2 to 49 mole $\%$ ZrF₄. It is characterized as a crystalline uniaxial substance that is optically negative with $N_{o} = 1.502$ and $N_{e} = 1.508$ , where $N_{o}$ and $N_{e}$ refer to the ordinary and extraordinary indices of refraction of uniaxial crystals.

Compositions containing about 50 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ which have been cooled at intermediate rates occasionally contain NaF. $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ as a nonequilibrium phase that is uniaxial positive with $N_{o} = 1.417$ and

![](images/32672289aaabc20a719c2ac7d6abeffb37bd799c736235e261ee73545e939b2a.jpg)  
Fig. 2.1.3. Thermal-Gradient Quenching Apparatus.

![](images/66843c69126d1300f580e189532c8265ff49d9bfa300251ae50002294f2ea7b3.jpg)  
Fig. 2.1.4. Rolling and Crimping Machine for Preparing Thermal-Gradient Quenching Samples.

$N_{e} = 1.446$ . This is rare, however, in slowly cooled fuel mixtures.

At higher NaF concentrations than 53.8 mole $\%$ crystals of the solid solution between $7\mathrm{NaF}\cdot 6\mathrm{ZrF}_4$ and $3\mathrm{NaF}\cdot 2\mathrm{ZrF}_4$ are observed which are, in general, poorly crystallized and which may be distinguished from $7\mathrm{NaF}\cdot 6\mathrm{ZrF}_4$ crystals by their lowered indices of refraction, their crystalline character, and their poor extinction when examined with crossed polarizing disks.

Oxygen will, in general, be observed as the oxyfluoride, which is uniaxial negative with indices of refraction greater than 1.54 to 1.7 and which frequently crystallizes as hexagonal plates. Zirconium oxide has been observed, and it is easily seen in samples of this type because of its pronounced angular cleavages, frequent high birefringence, and very high refractive indices (higher than 2.13).

The addition of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ may introduce, as a new material, a $\mathsf{UF}_4 - \mathsf{ZrF}_4$ solid solution which occurs in mixtures containing less than 50 mole % NaF and is biaxial negative $(2V = 50\deg)^{11}$ with marked birefringence and refractive indices of around 1.6. If $\mathsf{UO}_2$ is present it is optically isotropic and garnet red in color. Partial reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ to $\mathsf{UF}_3$ results in a yellow-brown cast to the otherwise green crystals.

# AN OPTICAL METHOD FOR THE DETERMINATION OF EQUILIBRIUM PATHS AND TIE LINES IN TERNARY FUSED-SALT SYSTEMS

C.F.Weaver

# Systems Without Solid Solution

If the liquidus temperatures and the boundary curves in a ternary phase equilibrium system are known, and if there is no solid solution, all the tie lines for any over-all composition and temperature can be drawn. The tie lines will then describe the equilibrium path (a straight line) and indicate the composition of the liquid in equilibrium with a primary phase as a function of temperature and over-all composition.

# Systems With Solid Solution

If there is solid solution, knowledge of the liquidus temperatures and the boundary curves is insufficient to allow the tie lines to be drawn. The difference between the case without solid solution and the case with solid solution lies in properties of the primary phase. In the case of no solid solution, the composition of the primary phase is given on the phase equilibrium diagram, and it is independent of temperature. In the case of solid solution, the composition of the primary phase is not specified on the phase equilibrium diagram. In addition the composition of the primary phase is, in general, a function of temperature.

Under certain conditions the composition of the primary phase as a function of temperature can be determined experimentally for each over-all composition. This information together with the liquidus temperatures can be used to draw the tie lines. As in the case of no solid solution, the tie lines will describe the equilibrium path (in general, a curved line) and indicate the composition of the

liquid in equilibrium with a primary phase as a function of temperature and over-all composition.

# Index of Refraction vs Solid Solution Composition

If a series of fused-salt solid solutions of known composition are prepared, their indices of refraction can be measured by means of a microscope and suitable immersion oils. A hypothetical plot of index of refraction vs composition for a continuous fused-salt solid solution between A and B is given in Fig. 2.1.5.

![](images/48684fa69589e13de252b042daa513738f0c5203b740dc0895251e73ec6ec4ce.jpg)  
Fig. 2.1.5. Hypothetical Plot of Index of Refraction vs Composition for a Continuous Fused-Salt Solid Solution Between A and B.

In general, when the solid solution AB appears in the system of interest its composition is not immediately known. It is readily apparent that the composition can be determined from Fig. 2.1.5, if the indices of refraction for the solid solution AB are measured. In order to avoid ambiguity, plots such as Fig. 2.1.5 must contain no maximums or minimums. Further it should be noted that the accuracy of the determination increases with the slope of the curve of index of refraction vs composition of the solid solution.

# Composition as a Function of Temperature

The composition of the solid solution AB must be determined, as described above, for a series of temperatures at a given overall composition $P$ in order to obtain the information needed for constructing the equilibrium paths. The composition of AB is plotted vs temperature, as in Fig. 2.1.6,

and the curve is valid, of course, only at point $P$ . Hence a series of such curves must be constructed to suitably cover the primary phase area of interest.

# Constructing Tie Lines and Equilibrium Paths

If the overall composition is considered to be that shown in Fig. 2.1.7 and it is assumed that Fig. 2.1.6 gives the solid solution composition

![](images/02bd2f75e56b1fa52bff00393cfd03f4cb5268c83c257258dfed9cd5efd83391.jpg)  
Fig. 2.1.6. Composition of AB vs Temperature for Point P.

![](images/ad2183d782342ffc1aec9977bfffebe3945e485d346becaa617cfce64a7340af.jpg)  
Fig. 2.1.7. Over-All Composition of Ternary System ABC.

vs temperature plot for $P_{\star}$ at temperature 1 the composition of AB solid solution is $a,$ the composition of the liquid is $P_{\star}$ and the tie line is $Pa$ Likewise for temperatures 2,3,and 4,the composition of AB solid solution is $b,c,$ and $d,$ the composition of the liquid is $b^{\prime},c^{\prime}$ and $d^{\prime}$ ,and the tie lines are $b^{\prime}b,c^{\prime}c,$ and $d^{\prime}d,$ respectively. The locus of all points such as $P,b^{\prime},c^{\prime}$ and $d^{\prime}$ is the equilibrium path.

# The System LiF-ThF4-UF4

The system $\mathsf{LiF - ThF}_{4} - \mathsf{UF}_{4}$ appears to contain three continuous solid solutions: $\mathsf{UF}_{4} - \mathsf{ThF}_{4}$

LiF·4UF4-LiF·4ThF4, and 7LiF·6UF4-7LiF·6ThF4. In the UF4-ThF4 solid solution the plot of index of refraction vs composition has a small slope. As previously mentioned, tie lines cannot be accurately determined when the slope of the plot is small. In the remaining two solid solutions, however, the slopes are sufficient for the determination of tie lines. These solid solutions offer ample opportunity for a study of the tie lines and equilibrium paths, and such studies have been initiated.

# 2.2. CHEMICAL REACTIONS IN MOLTEN SALTS

F. F. Blankenship

L.G.Overholser

R. F. Newton

G.M.Watson

# THE PREPARATION OF $\mathsf{UF}_3$ AND ITS STABILITY IN MOLTEN FLUORIDE MIXTURES

# F. F. Blankenship

The use of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in fuel mixtures was found to be impractical because disproportionation occurred and uranium metal deposited on the container surfaces. The work that led to this conclusion and the results of investigations of methods for eliminating or avoiding disproportionation are summarized here. The mechanism for disproportionation has been ascertained and means for controlling it are suggested. Petrographic and x-ray diffraction examinations were used in this study to characterize phases in solidified melts, and liquid compositions were, in general, defined by synthesis or by filtration and chemical analysis.

The $\mathsf{UF}_3$ compound may be prepared in solution by various redox reactions or by electrolysis, but, when large quantities were needed for corrosion studies, solid $\mathsf{UF}_4$ was used as the starting material. The reaction

$$
3 U F _ {4} + U ^ {\circ} \Longleftrightarrow 4 U F _ {3} \tag {1}
$$

was used to produce routinely kilogram batches of $99\% \mathsf{UF}_3$ . Essentially complete reaction of stoichiometric mixtures of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ and $\mathsf{U}^{\circ}$ was obtained in 48 hr at $900^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ .

The equilibrium pressure of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ above $\mathsf{UF}_3$ as a result of disproportionation by the reverse reaction in Eq. 1 was measured at temperatures between 1270 and $1400^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ in tantalum-lined vapor pressure apparatus. The results obtained are represented by the expression

$$
\log p (\mathrm {m m} \mathrm {H g}) = - \frac {4 1 8 7}{T} + 3. 9 4 5,
$$

where $T$ is in degrees Kelvin. The equilibrium values thus obtained indicated that crystalline $\mathsf{UF}_3$ is more stable than published estimates of the free energies of formation would indicate. In contrast, $\mathsf{UF}_3$ was found to disproportionate in molten fluoride solvents to a much greater extent than it did in the solid-crystal form. Partially complete phase diagrams were prepared $^6$ that were useful in approximating the solubility behavior of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in binary mixtures of alkali fluorides. The solubility of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF $_4$ and in NaF-BeF $_2$ , which are mixtures of interest as reactor fuels, is 1 to 2 wt% U at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

Nonacidic fuel solvents that contain a high concentration of fluoride donor constituents, such as alkali fluorides, were found to increase the extent of disproportionation by complexing the acidic $\mathsf{UF}_4$ (fluoride acceptor). The ease of alloy formation between the uranium formed and the container metal also noticeably influences the extent of the disproportionation. Less disproportionation occurred in copper vessels than in nickel vessels, and tantalum and molybdenum were found to be even more resistant than copper to alloying with the uranium formed.

It was also found that $\mathsf{UF}_3$ was oxidized by the alkali fluorides. The reaction was particularly noticeable with mixtures containing KF:

$$
\cup F _ {3} + K F \longrightarrow U F _ {4} + K ^ {\circ} \tag {2}
$$

For example, when a solution containing 21.4 wt % U as UF₃ in the LiF-NaF-KF eutectic (46.5-11.5-42 mole %) was heated under helium in copper apparatus, about one-third of the UF₃ was lost by disproportionation in 2 hr at either 650 or $750^{\circ}C$ ; the amount of UF₃ oxidized by KF at the same time increased from 7 to $18\%$ . Sufficient reduction in the helium pressure caused a rapid increase in the

rate of disappearance of $\mathsf{U}^{+ + + }$ . The sharp increase in the rate occurred at a pressure corresponding to the bubble pressure of potassium. The small apparent temperature coefficient of the disproportionation in such experiments was probably the result of the process being limited, after an initial rapid deposition of uranium, by the slow rate of diffusion of uranium into copper.

In comparison with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ -containing fuels, the $\mathsf{UF}_3$ -containing fuels exhibit a marked improvement in initial corrosion attack at high temperatures. Moreover, the use of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in conjunction with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ could provide reserve reducing capacity to compensate for any tendency of the fuel to become more oxidizing during burnup. Unfortunately the difficulties arising from the deposition of uranium by disproportionation prevent the easy exploitation of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ as a solution to corrosion problems. However, even in the $\mathsf{UF}_4$ fuels, some $\mathsf{UF}_3$ always appears as a product of the oxidizing action of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ , and the fate of this $\mathsf{UF}_3$ is of interest in connection with long reactor lives.

# ACTIVITY COEFFICIENTS OF $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ AND OF NiF 2 IN MOLTEN NaF-ZrF 4

C. M. Blood

The activity coefficients $^{11,12}$ of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ and of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ in NaF-ZrF $_4$ (53-47 mole %) were determined by comparing the values of the equilibrium quotients of the reaction

$$
\mathsf {M F} _ {2} (d) + \mathsf {H} _ {2} (g) \rightleftharpoons \mathsf {M} ^ {\circ} (s) + 2 \mathsf {H F} (g)
$$

with the corresponding values of equilibrium constants calculated from the tabulated free energies of formation of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ , $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ , and HF. Experimental values for the equilibrium quotients for the reactions of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ and of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ were shown to be independent of concentration. For the $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ reaction the values of $K_{x}$ are $2.6 \pm 0.2$ , $0.63 \pm 0.04$ , and $0.097 \pm 0.007$ atm at 800, 700, and $600^{\circ}C$ , respectively. These values are in essential agreement with equilibrium constants calculated from

the standard free energies of formation of pure solid and supercooled $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ at these temperatures. Accordingly, the activity coefficients for $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ in this solvent are near unity, regardless of the choice of standard state. The numerical values of the activity coefficients of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ are, at 800, 700, and $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , 1.46, 2.20, and 3.28 with respect to the pure crystalline solid, 0.64, 0.66, and 0.66 with respect to liquid $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ , and unity with respect to the standard state of reference at infinite dilution.

Equilibrium quotients for the $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ reduction have been determined to be $21,900 \pm 2,000$ , $15,300 \pm 700$ , $11,000 \pm 600$ , and $7,600 \pm 400$ atm, as compared with the values 10.9, 7.4, 4.8, and 3.1 calculated from the free energies of formation of pure solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ at 625, 600, 575, and $550^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , respectively. The corresponding constants calculated for liquid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ are 43.8, 33.6, 25.2, and 18.4. The very large discrepancies between the calculated and experimental constants give rise to very high values of the activity coefficients of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ . By using the solid as the standard state the numerical values of the activity coefficients were found to be 2009, 2076, 2292, and 2468 at 625, 600, 575, and $550^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , respectively. With supercooled liquid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ as the standard state, the corresponding values of the activity coefficients are 500, 455, 436, and 413.

It is clear from the values of the activity coefficients that when $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ is dissolved in NaF $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ (53-47 mole %) it behaves in a manner vastly different from that predicted by the free energies of formation for solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ . This is somewhat surprising in view of the agreement shown to exist between the calculated and experimental behavior of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ in the same solvent. Accordingly, the results of these investigations confirm that the tabulated values of the free energies of formation of solid $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ , the tabulated melting point, and the estimated heat of fusion are adequate to predict the behavior of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ in the solvent $\mathsf{NaF-ZrF}_4$ (53-47 mole %). On the other hand the corresponding values for $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ are not at all suitable to describe its behavior in the same solvent.

In the case of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ the experimentally determined free energy of formation at $600^{\circ}C$ for the standard state of reference at infinite dilution was -135.4 kcal/mole, as compared with the tabulated values -137.5 and -134.5 for the solid and liquid states, respectively. Thus the experimental value for $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ was found to be between the predicted values for the solid and the liquid states. On the

other hand, the experimentally determined free energy of formation of the standard state of reference for $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ was found, at $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , to be -114.6 kcal/mole, while the tabulated value for the solid state is -127.9. As has been pointed out elsewhere,[14] at first glance it appears that the value of the free energy of formation of the solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ is in error by approximately 13 kcal. It is known, however, that the solubility of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ is quite low in this solvent,[15] 0.17 mole % at $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ ; and, if the saturating phase is pure $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ , an activity coefficient of 588 would be required to give unit activity at saturation. Since the saturating phase is not pure $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ but rather a complex fluoride, it is only possible to predict from the solubility an upper limit of 588 for the activity coefficient for solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ as the standard state. The upper limit of 588 corresponds to a value of -125.7 for the free energy of formation of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ . Conversely, it may be stated that the upper limit at $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ of the free energy of formation for solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ is -125.7 kcal/mole, as given by its solubility, the experimental $K_{x'}$ and the tabulated free energy of formation for gaseous HF. The magnitude of a more reasonable value will now be considered.

In order to obtain an activity coefficient in the neighborhood of unity with supercooled liquid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ as the standard state, the free energy of formation must be approximately $-114.6\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ at $600^{\circ}C$ . The free energy of formation of solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ at $600^{\circ}C,$ based on an assumed free energy of formation of liquid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ of $-114.6,$ may be computed according to the equation

$$
\Delta F _ {N i F _ {2} (s)} ^ {\circ} = \Delta F _ {N i F _ {2} (l)} ^ {\circ} - \Delta F _ {M e l t i n g}.
$$

If the heat of fusion is assumed to be independent of temperature, then

$$
\Delta F _ {N i F _ {2} (s)} ^ {\circ} = \Delta F _ {N i F _ {2} (l)} ^ {\circ} - \Delta S _ {M} \left(T _ {M} - T\right),
$$

where $\Delta S_{M} = \Delta H_{f} / T_{M}$ , the entropy of fusion, and $T_{M}$ is the melting point of solid NiF $_2$ .

In previous calculations,12 a heat of fusion of 8000 cal/mole and a melting point of $1300^{\circ}K$ for $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ were assumed, as suggested by Brewer.13

These values give a free energy of formation for solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ of $-117.2\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ . However, some recent work indicates that the melting point of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ is greater than $1500^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ (ref 16). If the entropy of fusion for $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ ( $8000 / 1375 = 5.82\mathsf{e}\mathsf{u}$ ) is assumed for $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ , but a melting point of $1800^{\circ}\mathsf{K}$ is used, a value of $-120.0\mathsf{kcal / mole}$ is obtained as a reasonable estimate of the free energy of formation of solid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ at $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ .

# SOLUBILITIES AND ACTIVITY COEFFICIENTS OF $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ AS FUNCTIONS OF SOLVENT COMPOSITION IN NoF-ZrF<sub>4</sub> MIXTURES

C. M. Blood

It has been postulated[17,18] that the activity coefficients of a solute in a fluoride solvent will reach a maximum at the isoelectric point or when the charge-to-distance ratio of the solute's positive ion is equal to the average or effective charge-to-distance $(Z / R)$ ratio of the positive ions of the solvent mixture. As the composition of the solvent is changed and its charge-to-distance ratio is made to differ from that of the solute, negative deviations from ideality become greater. On this basis a tentative acid-base scale for predicting isoelectric points of solutes in $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF_4}$ melts was prepared.[18] Since not much experimental evidence is available on the effect of solvent composition on solute activity coefficients, the proposed plot is only tentative, since no proven way exists to obtain the effective $Z / R$ of the solvent as a function of composition. In an effort to provide quantitative information, hydrogen-reduction equilibrium quotients of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ were measured in various $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF}_4$ mixtures. Activity coefficients were calculated, but, since no satisfactory value of the free energy of formation of either solid or liquid $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ is available, the data are presented (Table 2.2.1) as the ratio of the activity coefficient of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ in the solvent indicated to the activity coefficient of $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ in $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF}_4$ (53-47 mole%). In addition, the solubility data are given as a function of solvent composition.

Table 2.2.1. Equilibrium Quotients, Activity Coefficient Ratios, and Solubilities of NiF $_2$ in NaF-ZrF $_4$ Mixtures at $600^{\circ}$ C

*Based on activity coefficient of NiF₂ in NaF-ZrF₄ (53-47 mole %).

The experimental measurements of the 40 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ mixture have not been completed, and the data given in Table 2.2.1 for this composition may be revised. It is proposed to make measurements on the solvent containing 37 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ in an effort to locate the positions of the maximums of the activity coefficient and solubility. The present results indicate that the effective $Z / R$ average of the solvent may correspond closely to that predicted by the proposed acid-base scale.[18] It may also be noted that the value of the activity coefficient of the $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ solute is not dependent on the solubility; this confirms a previous conclusion that the precipitating phase is not $\mathsf{NiF}_2$ .

# REDUCTION OF UF4 BY CHROMIUM IN MOLTEN FLUORIDES

J.D.Redman

The possible use of Inconel or stainless steel as a material for containing $\mathsf{UF}_4$ -bearing fluoride mixtures raised numerous questions as to the effect of corrosion attack in the temperature range 600 to $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ . The most active metal present in these alloys is chromium, and consequently any fundamental information relating to the interaction of chromium with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ dissolved in representative fluoride mixtures should be applicable to an understanding of the corrosion of the alloys by the fluoride melts.

A summary of the results of studies of the reduction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ by chromium in various fluoride

reaction mediums is presented here, and data for the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with various other metals are given in the next section. Some data are also given for the reaction of UF $^4$ with Inconel, in which the activity of chromium is much less than unity.

As might be expected, the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with chromium is influenced by the reaction medium employed. The reaction which occurs in the $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ - and $\mathsf{BeF}_2$ -bearing melts studied may be written as

$$
2 U F _ {4} + C r ^ {\circ} \rightleftharpoons 2 U F _ {3} + C r F _ {2}
$$

The valency of the chromium compound has been established through numerous experiments, and it has also been demonstrated experimentally that the solubility of the uranium and chromium compounds involved is considerably greater than the concentrations encountered in these studies. The equilibrium quotient, $K_{x^{\prime}}$ , defined as

$$
K _ {x} = \frac {N _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}} ^ {2} \cdot N _ {\mathrm {C r F} _ {2}}}{N _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}} ^ {2}},
$$

where $N$ denotes mole fraction, may be evaluated for this system by adding a known concentration of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ to chromium and determining the resulting concentration of $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ .Side reactions that arise from impurities $(\mathsf{H}_2\mathsf{O},\mathsf{HF},\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + },\mathsf{Ni}^{+ + })$ must be eliminated or suppressed to such a degree that the chromium blank is small compared with the total chromium measured. The blanks in these studies ranged from 150 to 250 ppm Cr.

A filtration method was used in which the melt was equilibrated under designated conditions and a sample was withdrawn through a sintered-nickel filter and analyzed for chromium. Nickel apparatus was used in most cases, because early experimental work demonstrated that nickel is stable toward $\mathsf{UF}_4$ dissolved in fluoride mixtures. Equilibrium is attained in these systems fairly rapidly, 3 hr being sufficient in virtually all cases, although a longer period of heating was utilized for some studies.

The data given in Table 2.2.2 demonstrate that the equilibrium quotient, $K_{x'}$ , is essentially constant over the range of concentrations of $UF_4$ and $UF_3$ studied with NaF-ZrF $_4$ (50-50 mole %) as the

Table 2.2.2 Equilibrium Quotients for the Reaction $2\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_{4}(\mathsf{d}) + \mathsf{Cr}(\mathsf{c})\rightleftharpoons 2\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_{3}(\mathsf{d}) + \mathsf{Cr}\mathsf{F}_{2}(\mathsf{d})$ in NaF-ZrF4 (50-50 mole %) at 600 and $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$   

<table><tr><td colspan="2">Conditions of Equilibration</td><td colspan="2">Original Concentration (mole %)</td><td rowspan="2">Equilibrium Chromium Concentration (ppm)</td><td rowspan="2">Equilibrium Quotient,* Kx</td></tr><tr><td>Temperature (°C)</td><td>Time (hr)</td><td>UF4</td><td>UF3</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="4">600</td><td>3 or 5</td><td>4.1</td><td></td><td>2220 ± 120</td><td>3.2 ± 0.8</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>3.0</td><td>0.82</td><td>950</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.0</td><td>1.7</td><td>155</td><td>3</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>0.89</td><td>2.5</td><td>30</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="5">800</td><td>3 or 5</td><td>4.1</td><td></td><td>2550 ± 210</td><td>5.9 ± 1.8</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>3.2</td><td>0.92</td><td>1120</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.2</td><td>1.9</td><td>320</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>1.3</td><td>2.8</td><td>60</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.0</td><td></td><td>1450</td><td>4</td></tr></table>

*Standard deviations were obtained from 10 measurements at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and 13 measurements at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The other values for $K_{x}$ were obtained from the arithmetic mean of two to four measurements.

reaction medium. As anticipated, the chromium concentrations decrease as the $\mathsf{UF}_3$ concentration increases; this suggests that the extent of attack on chromium or chromium-bearing alloys may be decreased by the addition of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ . The reaction has a small positive temperature coefficient; an average heat of reaction of 6 kcal in the temperature range 600 to $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ is obtained from the values for $K_{x}$ . Values of 70 and 20 were obtained for the equilibrium constants at 600 and at $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ , respectively, by using the available values of the free energies of formation for $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ , $\mathsf{UF}_4$ , and $\mathsf{UF}_3$ . The corresponding equilibrium concentrations were 8100 and 7300 ppm. A comparison of these values with those given in Table 2.2.2 indicates that this system behaves in a manner that is far from ideal.

When dealing with melts in the NaF-LiF-KF system, the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with $\mathsf{Cr}^{\circ}$ cannot be precisely defined. A mixture of $\mathsf{Cr}^{++}$ and $\mathsf{Cr}^{+++}$ results, but the ratio of the two ions has not been accurately determined. Equilibrium quotients cannot, therefore, be evaluated, but, rather, the data are presented in terms of total dissolved chromium $(\mathsf{Cr}^{++} + \mathsf{Cr}^{+++})$ . The data obtained with NaF-LiF-KF as the reaction medium are summarized in Table 2.2.3. Again it may be observed that the

Table 2.2.3. Equilibrium Chromium Concentrations for the Reaction of Chromium with UF4 in NaF·LiF·KF (11,5-46,5-42 mole %) at 600 and at $800^{\circ}\mathbf{C}$   

<table><tr><td colspan="2">Conditions of Equilibration</td><td colspan="2">Original Concentration (mole %)</td><td colspan="2">Equilibrium Concentration*</td></tr><tr><td>Temperature (°C)</td><td>Time (hr)</td><td>UF4</td><td>UF3</td><td>U (wt %)</td><td>Cr (ppm)</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="4">600</td><td>3</td><td>2.30</td><td></td><td>12.0</td><td>1130</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.30</td><td></td><td>11.7</td><td>1050</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.00</td><td>0.24</td><td>11.0</td><td>205</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>2.00</td><td>0.24</td><td>11.4</td><td>250</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="6">800</td><td>3</td><td>2.30</td><td></td><td>11.2</td><td>2650</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.30</td><td></td><td>11.5</td><td>2750</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>2.00</td><td>0.24</td><td>11.1</td><td>1890</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>2.00</td><td>0.24</td><td>10.8</td><td>1750</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>1.10</td><td>1.20</td><td>10.2</td><td>140</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>0.70</td><td>1.60</td><td>10.8</td><td>40</td></tr></table>

\*Arithmetic mean of two to six measurements.

chromium concentrations decrease as the $\mathsf{UF}_3$ concentration increases. The large difference in chromium concentrations at 600 and at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ indicates that the heat of reaction in this medium is much greater than that calculated for the reaction in NaF-ZrF<sub>4</sub> and suggests that the mass transfer of chromium is more severe in the former case.

The chromium concentrations for equilibrium with Inconel can be calculated by using an activity value of 0.1 for chromium in the alloy and the chromium concentration values given in Table 2.2.2. The values thus obtained are 1050 and 1150 ppm at 600 and at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , respectively, in the reaction medium $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF_4\cdot UF_4}$ . These concentrations are much lower than that given for equilibrium with pure chromium at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and indicate that it should not be possible to deposit chromium at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ when $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF_4\cdot UF_4}$ melts of this general composition are circulated in Inconel loops with hot- and cold-leg temperatures of 600 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , respectively. Mass transfer of chromium would occur in loops of pure chromium under these conditions, and would occur in an Inconel loop if the deposited chromium were able to diffuse rapidly enough into the cold-zone material.

Similarly the data given in Table 2.2.3 may be used to calculate the equilibrium chromium concentration in the NaF-LiF-KF-UF $_4$ melt at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ when Inconel is the container material. Values of 1250 and 1500 ppm were obtained based on $\mathbf{Cr}^{+ + }$ and $\mathbf{Cr}^{+ + + }$ , respectively, and the equilibrium concentration is probably intermediate between the two values. Since these concentrations are greater than the value of 1100 ppm found for equilibrium with pure chromium at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , Inconel in contact with the NaF-LiF-KF-UF $_4$ melt will support a higher concentration of $\mathbf{Cr}^{+ + } + \mathbf{Cr}^{+ + + }$ in equilibrium at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ than pure chromium can support at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Thus chromium would be removed from Inconel in the hot zone and deposited as essentially pure chromium in the cold zone. Since no diffusion process is required in the cold zone, the rate of attack in the hot zone would be controlled by the rate of diffusion of chromium to the metal-salt interface. The chromium concentrations that result when Inconel is contacted with UF $_4$ dissolved in either the NaF-ZrF $_4$ or NaF-LiF-KF mixture are given in Table 2.2.4.

It may be noted that the chromium concentrations given in Table 2.2.4 are, in general, lower than

those for reaction with pure chromium. If 700 ppm is taken for the chromium concentration in the NaF-ZrF $_4$ -UF $_4$ melt at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and 0.1 is taken for the activity of chromium in Inconel, a value of $5 \times 10^{-5}$ may be calculated for $K_{x}$ . This value is about an order of magnitude less than that given in Table 2.2.2 for the equilibrium quotient obtained for pure chromium. The discrepancy undoubtedly results from an apparent equilibrium state in which the Inconel surface is depleted in chromium because of the relatively slow diffusion of chromium to the surface.

Values for the equilibrium quotients for the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with chromium at 600 and at $800^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ in various reaction mediums are presented in Table 2.2.5. An examination of the data for the alkali fluoride- $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ binary mixtures containing approximately 52 mole % of alkali fluoride shows a

decrease in $K_{x}$ upon changing from lithium to rubidium. This behavior is attributed to the change in the cation charge-to-separation ratio, $Z / R$ , and the resulting decrease in fluoride activity upon going from LiF to RbF while keeping a constant mole percentage of alkali fluoride. A similar effect is noted when the NaF content in the NaF-ZrF $_4$ binary is increased from 50 to 59 mole%. The results are also shown in Fig. 2.2.1, where $K_{x}$ is plotted against either the cation radius or the mole percentage of NaF. The change in $K_{x}$ with temperature, which is a measure of the heat of reaction, is probably a good index for the mass transfer of chromium. Thus, in considering the effect of the solvent for UF $_4$ on the corrosion of chromium-bearing alloys, the change in $K_{x}$ must be considered, as well as the equilibrium chromium concentrations. mixtures listed in Table 2.2.5, other solvents, namely, $\mathsf{LiF - BeF}_2\mathsf{-ThF}_4$ (67-23-10 mole %) and $\mathsf{LiF - ThF}_4$ (72-28 mole %), have been studied with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ concentrations in the range 0.1 to 0.2 mole %. The extent of reaction with chromium at these low $\mathsf{UF}_4$ concentrations is so limited, however, that no measurable difference was found between the chromium blanks and the chromium concentrations resulting from the reaction of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ with chromium.

# REACTION OF $UF_4$ WITH VARIOUS STRUCTURAL METALS IN MOLTEN FLUORIDES

J. D. Redman

The compatibility of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ dissolved in various fluoride mixtures with various metals having desirable structural properties, either as the metal or in alloy form, is of considerable importance in

the study of molten fluoride fuels for reactors. Many of the available alloys have either a nickel or an iron base, with chromium added to impart oxidation resistance and high-temperature strength. Such alloys include Inconel and the stainless steels. Nickel is stable toward $\mathsf{UF}_4$ dissolved in the fluoride mixtures of interest and consequently is a suitable base for alloys. Data are presented here which demonstrate that even though iron reacts with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ the extent of the reaction is sufficiently limited that iron might also serve as a suitable base for the structural material. Data given in the previous section, in comparison with the data presented here, show conclusively that chromium is more reactive with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ than is iron and indicate that the chromium present in Inconel and the stainless steels is the element most likely to react with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ and therefore to play the important role in the corrosion of these alloys by $\mathsf{UF}_4$ -bearing fluoride mixtures. The possibility of replacing chromium in alloys by other elements, such as molybdenum, niobium, tantalum, vanadium, and tungsten, led to a study of the compatibility of these materials with $\mathsf{UF}_4$ dissolved in either alkali fluoride mixtures or alkali fluoride- $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ mixtures.

A filtration method was employed in which the $\mathsf{UF}_4$ -bearing fluoride mixture was equilibrated in nickel equipment with the metal under study and a sample was removed through a sintered-nickel filter and analyzed for the desired metal. In the case of iron in $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ -bearing melts the reaction is believed to proceed as

$$
2 U F _ {4} + F e ^ {\circ} \rightleftharpoons 2 U F _ {3} + F e F _ {2}
$$

and equilibrium quotients may be evaluated from the expression

$$
K _ {x} = \frac {N _ {\cup F _ {3}} ^ {2} \cdot N _ {F e F _ {2}}}{N _ {\cup F _ {4}} ^ {2}}.
$$

The valence states of the oxidation products of the metals other than iron have not been established, and consequently only the equilibrium metal concentrations are given.

The data presented in Table 2.2.6 show a remarkable insensitivity of iron concentration to changes in the composition of the reaction medium. It may be noted that the reaction exhibits a negative

Table 2.2.6. Data for the Reduction of $UF_4$ by Iron in Molten Fluorides at 600 and at $800^{\circ}C$   

<table><tr><td>Reaction Medium</td><td>Temperature (°C)</td><td>Fe (ppm)</td><td>Kx</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">LiF-ZrF4(52-48 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>500</td><td>1.4 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>370</td><td>5 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">NaF-ZrF4(53-47 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>490</td><td>2 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>380</td><td>6 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">KF-ZrF4(52-48 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>540</td><td>3 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>320</td><td>6 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">RbF-ZrF4(52-48 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>490</td><td>4 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>140</td><td>5 × 10-8</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">NaF-ZrF4(50-50 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>450</td><td>1 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>310</td><td>3 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">NaF-ZrF4(59-41 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>530</td><td>1.5 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>450</td><td>8 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">LiF-NaF-ZrF4(55-22-23 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>420</td><td>6 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>625</td><td>2 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">NaF-LiF-KF(11.5-46.5-42 mole %)</td><td>600</td><td>690</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>800</td><td>480</td><td></td></tr></table>

temperature coefficient in all mediums except the LiF-NaF-ZrF $^4$ mixture. The results given for the LiF-NaF-KF mixture, a composition quite different from the alkali fluoride- $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ mixtures, indicate a negative temperature coefficient and equilibrium iron concentrations very similar to those found in the $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ -bearing melts. In contrast to these results for iron, the behavior of chromium, as described above, follows the expected pattern in the alkali fluoride- $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ melts.

An examination of the data presented for various metals in Tables 2.2.7 through 2.2.11 reveals some interesting differences in the behavior of the individual metals and the effects of different reaction mediums. The only metal that shows any real stability in an NaF-LiF-KF-UF4 melt is molybdenum, which undergoes rather limited attack. The attack on molybdenum by an NaF-ZrF4-UF4 melt is less than that of the alkali fluoride mixture,

as is, in general, the case for most of the metals studied. Niobium is not stable in the NaF-LiF-KF-UF4 melt, but it is unattacked by either the NaF-ZrF4-UF4 or LiF-NaF-ZrF4-UF4 melt. Tantalum, which would be expected to behave similarly to niobium, is not stable in either type of melt. Vanadium, likewise, is not stable in any of the melts used, whereas tungsten is stable in the NaF-ZrF4-UF4 melt but not in the NaF-LiF-KF-UF4 melt. In general, the extent of attack on the metals increases with a temperature increase from 600 to $800^{\circ}C$ .

It is evident that only molybdenum exhibits any stability toward $\mathsf{UF}_4$ in the NaF-LiF-KF mixture, and accordingly nickel-molybdenum alloys would be expected to resist corrosion by this type of melt. In the $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ -based melts, molybdenum, niobium, and tungsten appear to be stable toward $\mathsf{UF}_4$ , and thus these metals or their alloys might serve as suitable container materials for this type of melt. Tantalum and vanadium appear to offer no advantage over chromium for either type of melt.

# REDUCTION OF $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ BY CHROMIUM IN MOLTEN FLUORIDES

J. D. Redman

A study has been made of the reaction

$$
F e F _ {2} + C r ^ {\circ} \rightleftharpoons F e ^ {\circ} + C r F _ {2}
$$

in an attempt to evaluate the equilibrium quotient

$$
K _ {x} = \frac {N _ {\mathrm {C r F} _ {2}}}{N _ {\mathrm {F e F} _ {2}}}
$$

at $600^{\circ}C$ in the following reaction mediums: NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 mole %), KF-ZrF4 (52-48 mole %), and RbF-ZrF4 (52-48 mole %). Although it was known that the equilibrium quotient for this reaction was much greater than 1, it was believed that the equilibrium concentrations of $\mathrm{Cr^{+ + }}$ and $\mathrm{Fe^{+ + }}$ might be determined sufficiently accurately by the filtration method to permit an evaluation of the ratio.

If the system behaves ideally, a value of approximately $6 \times 10^{3}$ for $K_{a}$ is obtained from the standard free energies of formation for $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ and $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ at $600^{\circ}C$ , and thus essentially complete reduction of $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ by the chromium is indicated. A calculation of the $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ -to- $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ ratio from values found for $K_{x}$

Table 2.2.7. Data for the Reaction of ${\mathrm{{UF}}}_{4}$ with Molybdenum in Molten Fluorides at 600 and at ${800}^{ \circ  }\mathrm{C}$

*Blanks of 20 ppm were present at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in both reaction mediums and have been subtracted from the amount found in the filtrate.

for the reaction

$$
M ^ {\circ} + 2 U F _ {4} \rightleftharpoons 2 U F _ {3} + M F _ {2}
$$

where $M^{\circ}$ is iron or chromium, in the NaF-ZrF $_4$ mixture at $600^{\circ}C$ yielded a value of $\sim 60$ , however, which suggests that the behavior is far from ideal. The corresponding values in the KF-ZrF $_4$ and RbF-ZrF $_4$ mixtures are $\sim 10$ and $\sim 3$ , respectively. These values for the $\mathrm{Cr^{++}}$ -to- $\mathrm{Fe^{++}}$ ratios, if correct, should be determinable by the filtration method.

The data given in Table 2.2.12 were obtained from filtration runs in nickel equipment. In most cases the equilibration time was 5 hr. Several runs, with an equilibration period of 12 hr gave similar values and demonstrated that equilibrium was

attained within 5 hr. A comparison of the experimentally determined chromium values with the theoretical values shows that, in the case of the NaF-ZrF $_4$ and KF-ZrF $_4$ mixtures, the reaction occurs as indicated and yields a good balance between the Fe++ added and the Cr++ found, except at the higher concentrations. The abnormal behavior at the higher concentrations will be discussed below. In the data for the runs with RbF-ZrF $_4$ as the reaction medium, it may be noted that the calculated chromium values are about 10 to 20% lower than the experimentally determined values, in most cases. No experimental error could be found to explain this discrepancy, and, although there is no evidence that the valence of the iron and chromium is not 2, some abnormal behavior must occur.

*Blank of 130 ppm present at $800^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ in the NaF-ZrF $_4$ mixture; 650 ppm present in the NaF-LiF-KF mixture at $800^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ .

The reduction of $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ was essentially complete in all cases, except at the higher $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ additions in the NaF-ZrF4 mixture. In fact the $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ concentrations are so low that the ratio of $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ to $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ cannot be evaluated in any of the reaction mediums. A value of $\sim 60$ was calculated, as stated above, for the ratio in the NaF-ZrF4 mixture, and the experimental data do not deny that it may be this large. However, for the KF-ZrF4 and RbF-ZrF4 mixtures, it is evident that the experimentally determined ratios, while not constant, are considerably greater than the calculated ratios of $\sim 10$ and $\sim 3$ . This lack of agreement must be attributed to some uncertainty in the $K_{x}$ values found for the iron and chromium reactions with UF4.

The limited solubility of $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ in the NaF-ZrF4 mixture precludes the addition of large amounts of FeF, and thus the concentration range is rather

limited. It will be noted that for runs made at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ with either 1 or $2\%$ additions of $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ the results are not precise and very incomplete reduction of $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ occurred. This behavior probably results from the presence of solid phases containing NaF, $\mathsf{ZrF}_{4},\mathsf{CrF}_{2},$ and/or $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ . The solid phase contains more $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ than NaF, and consequently the liquid phase is enriched with respect to NaF. The lack of precision indicates that equilibrium was not attained in these runs. It is of interest to compare these runs with similar runs made at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in which the solubility of the $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ -bearing compound was not exceeded. Here essentially complete reduction of $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ occurred, and a stoichiometric relationship prevailed between the $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ added and the $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ found. It is apparent that the method employed is incapable of establishing the $\mathsf{Cr}^{+ + }$ -to- $\mathsf{Fe}^{+ + }$ ratio in these reaction mediums at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

# THERMODYNAMIC INTERPRETATION OF EQUILIBRIUM DATA ON THE CORROSION OF IRON AND OF CHROMIUM BY MOLTEN-SALT FUELS

M. Blander

Considerable attention has been given to the corrosion and metal deposition caused by the temperature coefficient of the equilibrium<sup>19</sup>

$$
2 U F _ {4} + M \rightleftharpoons 2 U F _ {3} + M F _ {2} \tag {1}
$$

when molten-salt mixtures are used as the reaction mediums. The equilibrium constant for the reaction is

$$
K _ {\alpha} = \frac {a _ {M F _ {2}} a _ {U F _ {3}}}{a _ {U F _ {4}} ^ {2} a _ {M}},
$$

where $a$ is the activity of the designated material, the salts are dissolved in the fuel melt, and the metal is in the container wall. Some of the differences in the behavior of structural metals are very important from the standpoint of compatibility between fuel and container. The effect of temperature,[20] for a steady-state concentration of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ , $\mathsf{UF}_3$ , and $\mathsf{MF}_2$ in the melt, can be represented as

$$
\begin{array}{l} R T ^ {2} \frac {d \ln X _ {M}}{d T} = - (\Delta H ^ {\circ} + \Delta H _ {s}) \tag {2} \\ = - R T ^ {2} \frac {d \ln K _ {x}}{d T}, \\ \end{array}
$$

where $X_{M}$ is the equilibrium concentration of metal M in the wall, $\Delta H^{\circ}$ is the heat of change of the reactants in the equilibrium reaction (Eq. 1) from their standard state (chosen here as the pure liquids for the salts and pure solid for the metal), $\Delta H_{s}$ is the difference between the heats of solution of the reactants and products, and $K_{x}$ is the equilibrium quotient:

$$
K _ {x} = \frac {N _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {3}} ^ {2} N _ {\mathrm {M F} _ {2}}}{N _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}} ^ {2} N _ {\mathrm {M}}}
$$

where $N$ is the mole fraction of the designated material. If $\Delta H^{\circ} + \Delta H_{s}$ is positive the metal M will tend to transfer from the hot to the cold end of

the system and, if negative, from the cold end to the hot end. The most desirable condition would be for $\Delta H^{\circ} + \Delta H_{s}$ to be zero so that there would be no mass transfer by the proposed mechanism.

If the pure liquids involved in the reaction are chosen as the standard state, the values of $\Delta H^0$ tabulated below can be obtained by using the heat content values listed in Table 2.2.13:

<table><tr><td>M</td><td>ΔHo</td></tr><tr><td>Cr</td><td>+9</td></tr><tr><td>Fe</td><td>+22</td></tr><tr><td>Ni</td><td>+33</td></tr></table>

Since all these values of $\Delta H^{\circ}$ are positive, there would be a tendency for mass transfer to take place from the hot to the cold end of the system if the $\Delta H^{\circ}$ term is not compensated for by the term $\Delta H_{s}$ , which involves all the heats of solution.

The term $\Delta H_{s}$ is given by

(3) $\Delta H_{s} = 2(\bar{H}_{\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_{3}} - H_{\mathsf{U}\mathsf{F}_{3}}^{\circ}) + (\bar{H}_{\mathsf{M}\mathsf{F}_{2}} - H_{\mathsf{M}\mathsf{F}_{2}}^{\circ})-$

$$
- 2 \left(\bar {H} _ {\cup F _ {4}} - H _ {\cup F _ {4}} ^ {\circ}\right) - \left(\bar {H} _ {M} - H _ {M} ^ {\circ}\right),
$$

where $H$ is the partial molal heat content of the material in solution and $H^0$ is the molal heat content of the pure substance. For a pure metal the last term in Eq. 3 is zero. The other terms in this expression are unknown, but if data are available

Table 2.2.13. Heat Content Data* for Reactants of the Reaction $2\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_{4} + \mathbf{M} = 2\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_{3} + \mathbf{M}\mathbf{F}_{2}$   

<table><tr><td>Compound</td><td>Heat of Formation, ΔH298 (kcal)</td><td>Heat of Fusion, ΔHf(kcal)</td></tr><tr><td>UF4</td><td>-443</td><td>5.7</td></tr><tr><td>UF3</td><td>-357</td><td>8.5</td></tr><tr><td>CrF2</td><td>-181</td><td>(12)**</td></tr><tr><td>NiF2</td><td>-159.5</td><td>(12)**</td></tr><tr><td>FeF2</td><td>-168</td><td>(12)**</td></tr></table>

*From Selected Values of Chemical Thermodynamic Properties, NBS Circular 500.   
\*\*Estimated by using a method discussed by Blander and Blankenship in a previous report, ANP Quar. Prog. Rep. Sept. 30, 1957, ORNL-2387, p 121.

they can be obtained from the equation

$$
\left[ \frac {d \ln \gamma}{d (1 / T)} \right] _ {N} = + \frac {\bar {H} - H ^ {\circ}}{R}, \tag {4}
$$

where $\gamma$ is the activity coefficient based on pure liquid as the standard state and the subscript $N$ denotes that the mole fractions are held constant. A comparison can be made of the behavior of two different pure metals, such as Cr and Fe, in any given melt composition that is relatively dilute in $\mathrm{CrF}_2$ or $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ . Since the values for the heats of solution of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ are the same for a melt dilute in $\mathrm{CrF}_2$ as for a melt dilute in $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ , and similarly for $\mathrm{UF}_4$ , it follows from Eq. 2 that

(5) $RT^2 \frac{d \ln (N_{\mathsf{Cr}} / N_{\mathsf{Fe}})}{dT} = -\Delta H_{\mathsf{CrF}_2}^0 +$

$$
\begin{array}{l} + \Delta H _ {\text {F e F} _ {2}} ^ {\circ} - (\bar {H} - H ^ {\circ}) _ {\text {C r F} _ {2}} + (\bar {H} - H ^ {\circ}) _ {\text {F e F} _ {2}} \\ = \frac {R T ^ {2} d \ln \left[ K _ {x} (\mathrm {F e}) / K _ {x} (\mathrm {C r}) \right]}{d T}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The values of $K_{x}$ for chromium and for iron in NaF-ZrF $_4$ (53-47 mole %) at 600 and at 800°C (ref 21) are given in Table 2.2.14.

From the similarity of the compounds $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ and $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ it would be expected that their solution behaviors would not differ greatly and that any difference would be governed mainly by the difference of their $\Delta H^0$ values. As may be seen in Table 2.2.14, these suppositions are not valid. It must be concluded either that some of the data are

Table 2.2.14. Equilibrium Quotients and Heat Changes Derived from Temperature Coefficients for Chromium and Iron in NaF-ZrF $_4$ (53-47 Mole %)   

<table><tr><td></td><td>Chromium</td><td>Iron</td></tr><tr><td>KxAt 600°C</td><td>1.3 × 10-4</td><td>2 × 10-6</td></tr><tr><td>At 800°C</td><td>2.9 × 10-4</td><td>6 × 10-7</td></tr><tr><td>ΔH° + ΔHs</td><td>7.5 kcal</td><td>-11.3 kcal</td></tr><tr><td>ΔH°</td><td>9 kcal</td><td>22 kcal</td></tr></table>

incorrect or that $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ and $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ have entirely different solution behavior.

Speculations as to the source of the discrepancy may be made on the basis of available data and some reasonable estimates for the heats of solution. From data on the activity coefficients of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ (ref 22) based on the solid as the standard state and the estimate of the heat of fusion of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ given in Table 2.2.13, the heat of solution of $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ was found to be $-4\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ . Since the heat of solution of $\mathsf{UF}_4$ is probably similar to that of $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ (ref 23) in the fuel melt, it is assumed to be between 2.0 and $4.3\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ . Similarly, it is likely that the heat of solution of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in the fuel melt lies in the range $\pm 5\mathrm{kcal}$ . If these heats of solution are combined with the values of $\Delta H$ given in Table 2.2.13, a range of values for $(\Delta H^{\circ} + \Delta H_{s})$ of $+15$ to $+27\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ is obtained for the $\mathsf{FeF}_2$ equilibrium; the apparent value of $-11\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ given in Table 2.2.14 seems to be improbable. It may be considered therefore that the measured temperature coefficient of the iron equilibrium reaction appears to be unreasonable. The probable range for $(\Delta H^{\circ} + \Delta H_{s})$ for the $\mathsf{CrF}_2$ equilibrium is $+2$ to $+14\mathrm{kcal / mole}$ . Within the broad limits of the method of estimation used here the temperature coefficient of $K_{x}$ for the chromium equilibrium is not unreasonable.

# SOLUBILITIES OF NOBLE GASES IN MOLTEN FLUORIDE MIXTURES

N.V. Smith

The solubilities of noble gases in molten salts have been investigated because such gases are produced in fused-salt-fueled reactors. Xenon gas is an important reactor poison, and the ease of its removal from the reactor core depends primarily on its absolute solubility in the particular fused salt mixture used as the fuel. A knowledge of the temperature dependence of the solubility was needed in order to predict in what portion of the reactor system the gas would have the greatest tendency to come out of solution.

Helium and argon gases are used as protective blankets and for sparging during reactor operations, and therefore their solubilities in fuel mixtures

were also studied. It was conceivable that a portion of the reactor fuel could become saturated with these gases in one section of a reactor and supersaturated in another and that bubble formation could result. This condition could lead to undesirable perturbations in reactivity. The solubilities of helium and of argon were determined as functions of pressure and temperature.

Because of the chemical simplicity of the noble gases, the solubility relationships of these gases in molten salts may be correlated with some fundamental aspects of liquid structure and chemical composition. The variations of the solubilities, the heats and entropies of solution, and the other properties with the atomic size of the gas may perhaps be sufficiently meaningful to reveal some of the characteristics of the potential-energy functions that govern the behavior of the ionic and molecular species in the liquid.

A summary of the results obtained thus far $^{24-26}$ in the determination of the solubilities of various noble gases in a number of different solvents is presented in Table 2.2.15. In all the cases investigated, Henry's law was found to apply. In addition, the solubilities were found to increase with increasing temperature. For the same solvent, the solubilities of the gases decreased with increasing solute gas size. Variations in solvents showed corresponding gas solubilities to be approximately the same in the two related $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ -bearing solvents, but the solubility of helium in NaF-KF-LiF was found to be less than half that in the $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ -bearing solvents.

The heats and entropies of solution of the four gases studied in the NaF-ZrF $_4$ (53-47 mole %) mixture are summarized below:

(kcal/mole)</td><td>ΔS

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Solvent</td><td rowspan="2">Gas</td><td colspan="3">K*</td></tr><tr><td>At 600°C</td><td>At 700°C</td><td>At 800°C</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>×10-8</td><td>×10-8</td><td>×10-8</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="4">NaF-ZrF4(53-47 mole %)</td><td>Helium</td><td>21.6</td><td>29.2</td><td>42.0</td></tr><tr><td>Neon</td><td>11.3</td><td>18.4</td><td>24.7</td></tr><tr><td>Argon</td><td>5.06</td><td>8.07</td><td>12.0</td></tr><tr><td>Xenon</td><td>1.9</td><td>3.6</td><td>6.3</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">NaF-ZrF4-UF4(50-46-4 mole %)</td><td>Helium</td><td>20</td><td>27</td><td>41</td></tr><tr><td>Xenon</td><td>2.0</td><td>4.0</td><td>6.5</td></tr><tr><td>NaF-KF-LiF (11.5-42-46.5 mole %)</td><td>Helium</td><td>11.3</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

$*K = c / p$ in moles of gas per cubic centimeter of melt per atmosphere.

It may be observed that the heat of solution increases with increasing atomic size of the gas. Also it is worth noting that the entropy of solution corrected to equal concentrations is negative and in all cases quite small. Because of the uncertainties of the measurements, the heats of solution are considered to be reliable only to $\pm 1$ kcal/mole and the entropy changes are reliable only to $\pm 1$ eu. Accordingly it appears that the trend in the heats of solution is real but that no particular significance should be attached to the different values of the entropy change, since as far as the experimental measurements are concerned the values listed for the entropy change are not significantly different.

# SOLUBILITY OF HF IN NaF-ZrF4 J.H.Shaffer

It may be noted that the solubility of HF, in all cases, decreases with increasing temperature.

As may be noted, the solubility of HF undergoes an inflection point at approximately 44 mole $\%$ $\mathsf{ZrF}_4$ , and it increases with increasing NaF concentration.

Attempts to correlate the observed changes of HF solubility with corresponding changes in solvent composition will be withheld until considerably more information is available. Experiments to measure the solubility of HF in NaF-KF-LiF (11.5-42-46.5 mole %) and in LiF-BeF $_2$ and NaF-BeF $_2$ mixtures are now under way.

# SOLUBILITIES OF FISSION-PRODUCT FLUORIDES IN MOLTEN NaF-ZrF<sub>4</sub> MIXTURES

W. T. Ward

R.A.Strehlow

Experiments have been completed in order to determine (1) the effect that varying the ratio of sodium to zirconium in the $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF_4}$ solvent has on the solubility of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ , (2) the solubility of $\mathsf{YF}_3$ in $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF}_4$ (53-47 mole %), and (3) the solubilities of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ and $\mathsf{SmF}_3$ when both are present in NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 mole %). The solubility measurements were made by using the radiochemical tracer techniques described previously.[29,30]

# $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF4

It has been found that varying the ratio of sodium to zirconium in the solvent has a considerable effect on the solubility of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ . This effect is clearly shown in Fig. 2.2.5, in which the solubility vs temperature curves for five different $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF}_4$ compositions are plotted. In addition to the compositions indicated on Fig. 2.2.5, the $\mathsf{NaF - ZrF}_4$ (80.5-19.5 mole %) solvent was briefly investigated. It was found that all the 4.60 mole % $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ that was added was soluble at temperatures from 793 to $915^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ .

![](images/fdbe3d091b78bd75e26b47ffd272dc0b466de8c6ec642c63df46fce1b2308903.jpg)  
Fig. 2.2.5. Solubility vs Temperature for $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ in Various NoF-ZrF4 Mixtures.

One interesting observation is that in the solvent NaF-ZrF $_4$ (42-58 mole %) the nature of the precipitating phase changes at about $640^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the inflection point on the curve. A plot of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ solubility at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ as a function of composition is presented in Fig. 2.2.6, and it may be noted that the curve is quite similar to that obtained earlier for the solubility of $\mathsf{UF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF $_4$ . Such correlation would be expected from a consideration

![](images/df10c64fb343418e929eb87865d6c2d1713f71fc9dd8d0eef3d97ba2c57d7e1f.jpg)  
Fig. 2.2.6. Solubility of $\mathrm{CeF}_3$ at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in NaF-ZrF $_4$ Mixtures as a Function of Composition.

of the similarity of the $U^{+ + + }$ and the $\mathsf{Ce}^{+ + + }$ ionic radii, that of $U^{+ + + }$ being 1.03 Å and that of $\mathsf{Ce}^{+ + + }$ being 1.02 Å. It is quite likely that an inflection exists in the solubility curve at a composition near 41 mole % $\mathsf{ZrF}_{4}$ , since with higher NaF content the precipitating phase is not $\mathsf{CeF}_{3}$ but rather $\mathsf{NaCeF}_{4}$ , as determined by petrographic observation.

# YF3 in NaF-ZrF4

The solubility of $\mathsf{YF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 mole %) was determined, and the results are presented in Table 2.2.17 and Fig. 2.2.7. Although some

Table 2.2.17. $YF_{3}$ Solubility in NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 Mole %)   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Filtration Temperature (℃)</td><td colspan="2">Solubility</td></tr><tr><td>In wt % Y</td><td>In mole % YF3</td></tr><tr><td>854</td><td>11.37</td><td>13.68</td></tr><tr><td>817</td><td>8.68</td><td>10.26</td></tr><tr><td>756</td><td>6.31</td><td>7.39</td></tr><tr><td>663</td><td>4.10</td><td>4.75</td></tr><tr><td>560</td><td>2.37</td><td>2.72</td></tr></table>

scattered earlier results had indicated that the plot of the log of the solubility vs the reciprocal of the temperature for $\mathsf{YF}_3$ was linear, these results show a behavior pattern more similar to the patterns obtained for the rare earths.

# $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ plus $\mathsf{SmF}_3$ in NaF-ZrF4

The solubilities of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ and $\mathsf{SmF}_3$ in combination in NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 mole %) were measured by

![](images/eae9213c10a46f332f1b65a55315d012324aa140f1ab70c421f4515f866e0be3.jpg)  
Fig. 2.2.7. Solubility vs Temperature for $\mathbf{Y}\mathbf{F}_3$ in NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 Mole%).

using the two-tracer technique described previously.30 Filtrates were obtained over the temperature range of 550 to $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for two cerium-to-samarium ratios. A summary of the solubility data is presented in Table 2.2.18, along with the solubility of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ by itself in the same solvent. The solubility vs temperature relationships are illustrated in Fig. 2.2.8, where the logs of the sums of the solubilities are plotted vs the reciprocal of the temperature.

The solubility of pure $\mathsf{SmF}_3$ has not yet been measured in this solvent, but, by utilizing the correlation of solubility with the cube of the ionic radius, estimates were made of the solubilities of $\mathsf{SmF}_3$ at these temperatures. The values obtained are shown in Table 2.2.19.

These values are summarized in the pseudoternary diagram presented as Fig. 2.2.9. The rare-earth fluoride solubility behavior in this solvent is seen to be substantially the same as the behavior reported previously when the solvent was NaF-ZrF $_4$ -UF $_4$ (50-46-4 mole%).<sup>32</sup>

# SOLUBILITY OF BaO IN KF-LiF

# J.H.Shaffer

The solubility of BaO in KF-LiF (50-50 mole %) is of interest in the systematic investigation of possibilities of removal of fission-product fluorides from NaF-LiF-KF base mixtures. Three experiments have been performed to obtain solubility values in which the starting barium compound, either $\mathsf{BaF}_2$ or BaO, was labeled with radioactive $\mathsf{Ba}^{133}$ .

![](images/04dc209594704995519b50de7c79f3bc3926525d4e441b3e005c0f1ff1685a97.jpg)  
Fig. 2.2.9. Pseudoternary Diagram of the System $\mathsf{CeF}_3\text{-SmF}_3$ with the Mixture NaF-ZrF4 (53-47 Mole %) as the Solvent.

The melting point and heat of fusion of BaO are listed $^{33}$ as $1923^{\circ}C$ and 13.8 kcal/mole, respectively. Accordingly the estimated solubility of BaO at $600^{\circ}C$ would be quite low (less than 0.5 mole%). Since it was considered to be difficult to prepare labeled BaO free of traces of soluble impurities, such as carbonates, hydroxides; and chlorides, it was decided to prepare the BaO in situ from $\mathsf{BaF}_2$ . The soluble impurities would cause apparent solubility values of BaO to be erroneously high. This preparation was attempted in two different experiments. The first consisted in the addition to the melt of LiOH containing a known amount of labeled $\mathsf{BaF}_2$ . A filtrate sample was obtained prior to the addition of the LiOH, and filtrates at 700 and $600^{\circ}C$ were obtained after the addition was completed. Since no change was noted in the concentration of barium in solution, it was decided to repeat the experiment with sodium peroxide as the precipitating agent. The results of the second experiment also showed no change in the concentration of barium in the liquid after addition of $20\%$ and of $140\%$ excess $\mathsf{Na}_2\mathsf{O}_2$ , calculated as $\mathsf{Na}_2\mathsf{O}$ .

In view of the unexpected results obtained in the first two experiments, a third experiment was carried out with labeled BaO as the additive. The labeled BaO was prepared by ignition of $\mathrm{Ba(NO_3)_2}$ . Again the results showed that the solubility of BaO was quite high. The labeled BaO used was examined with the petrographic microscope and by x-ray diffraction. The results of these examinations show that the material used was high-quality BaO. The numerical results of the three experiments are summarized in Table 2.2.20.

# REACTION OF $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ AND UF4 WITH CaO IN KF-LiF

J. H. Shaffer

Studies of the precipitation of cerium as $\mathrm{Ce}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{3}$ from the molten KF-LiF eutectic and of the solubility of barium oxide in this solvent (see preceding section) have provided a basis for investigating the possible separation of tetravalent metals as oxides from the trivalent fission products of a fused-salt reactor. Alternatives to this separation are to precipitate the rare earths as oxides and leave the uranium in the solvent or to precipitate uranium oxide and leave the fission products in solution.

In the present study, 5 wt % of labeled Ce was added as $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ to the molten KF-LiF eutectic

containing 1 mole $\%$ U as $\mathsf{UF}_4$ at $600^{\circ}\mathsf{C}$ . Calcium oxide was then added in tared increments, and filtrate samples were taken approximately 2 hr after each addition. These samples were counted for radioactivity to determine the cerium concentration and were chemically analyzed for uranium. The results are shown graphically in Fig. 2.2.10.

It may be noted that the cerium concentration increases, initially, because of the removal of the uranium from solution, until the equivalence point for the uranium is reached. Upon further addition

![](images/4c0b659a5e67a3068c9f2d5e78b018c0749fbf39f559f78fc105204302503a4b.jpg)  
Fig. 2.2.10. Precipitation of $\mathsf{CeF}_3$ and $\mathsf{UF_4}$ with CaO in KF-LiF (50-50 Mole%).

Table 2.2.20. Concentration of Barium in Liquid KF-LiFAfter Addition of $\mathsf{BaF}_2$ -LiOH, $\mathsf{BaF}_2$ -Na $_2$ O $_2$ , and BaO
